🏗️ Workload Resources

Workload resources are the core of Kubernetes, managing how your anihpj Django applications run. These resources control the lifecycle, scaling, and scheduling of your containerized anihpj Django applications across the cluster.

🚀 Essential Pod Components for Django

Pod - Basic deployable unit for Django containers
PodSpec - Defines desired Pod state and configuration
Container - Specifies anihpj Django application container details
Probe - Health and readiness checks for Django apps
Affinity - Controls Pod placement and co-location
Operations - CRUD operations for Pod management
💡 Django Best Practice: Use Pods directly for development and testing. In production, use Deployments to manage Pod replicas with rolling updates and high availability.

Pod

Docs: Kubernetes Pod v1 API

The smallest deployable unit in Kubernetes, representing one or more containers that share storage and network.

Full description

A Pod is the atomic unit of deployment in Kubernetes. For anihpj Django applications, a Pod typically contains your Django web server container, and optionally sidecar containers for logging, monitoring, or caching. Pods share an IP address, storage volumes, and lifecycle. When you deploy your anihpj jobpost Django application, each Pod runs your Django server and can access your models like Skills, Author, Location, and JobPost from the jobpost app. Pods are ephemeral - they can be created, destroyed, and recreated by higher-level controllers like Deployments.

Syntax

apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: pod-name
  namespace: namespace-name
  labels:
    app: app-name
    component: component-name
spec:
  containers:
  - name: container-name
    image: image:tag
    ports:
    - containerPort: port-number
    env:
    - name: ENV_VAR
      value: "value"
    resources:
      requests:
        memory: "memory-request"
        cpu: "cpu-request"
      limits:
        memory: "memory-limit"
        cpu: "cpu-limit"

Parameters description

  • apiVersion: Always "v1" for Pod resources. Specifies the Kubernetes API version to use for this resource.
  • kind: Must be "Pod" to indicate this is a Pod resource definition.
  • metadata.name: Unique name for the Pod within the namespace. Use descriptive names like "jobpost-web-pod" or "django-app-worker".
  • metadata.namespace: Kubernetes namespace where the Pod will be created. Defaults to "default" if not specified. Use separate namespaces for different environments.
  • metadata.labels: Key-value pairs for organizing and selecting Pods. Essential for Service discovery and Deployment management. Use consistent labeling like app=jobpost, component=web.
  • spec.containers: Array of container specifications. Most Django applications need only one container, but you can add sidecars for Redis, logging, or monitoring.
  • spec.containers[].name: Name of the container within the Pod. Use descriptive names like "django-web" or "jobpost-api".
  • spec.containers[].image: Docker image to run. Always include a specific tag for version control (e.g., "jobpost-django:v2.1.0").
  • spec.containers[].ports: Ports that the container exposes. Django typically uses port 8000 for development or 80/443 for production.
  • spec.containers[].env: Environment variables passed to the container. Include Django settings, database URLs, Redis URLs, and secret keys.
  • spec.containers[].resources: CPU and memory resource requests and limits. Requests guarantee resources, limits prevent overconsumption.

Return value

Pod object: Returns the created Pod resource with system-assigned fields including IP address, node placement, creation timestamp, and current status. The Pod will initially show phase "Pending" while being scheduled, then "Running" when containers start successfully, or "Failed" if there are issues.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Basic anihpj JobPost Pod
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: anihpj-web
  labels:
    app: anihpj
spec:
  containers:
  - name: anihpj-web
    image: anihpj:v1.0
    ports:
    - containerPort: 8000
pod/anihpj-web created Name: anihpj-web Status: Running IP: 10.244.1.15

Example 2: Django Pod with database connection
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: anihpj-db
spec:
  containers:
  - name: django-app
    image: anihpj:v1.0
    env:
    - name: DATABASE_URL
      value: "postgresql://user:pass@postgres-svc:5432/anihpj_db"
pod/anihpj-db created Status: Running Environment: DATABASE_URL configured
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Author.objects.select_related('user').all() &lt;QuerySet [&lt;Author: ABC Corp&gt;, &lt;Author: XYZ Inc&gt;, &lt;Author: 123 Industries&gt;, &lt;Author: Tech Solutions&gt;, &lt;Author: Tech Innovations&gt;]&gt;
Example 3: Django Pod with resource limits
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: anihpj-limited
spec:
  containers:
  - name: django-app
    image: anihpj:v1.0
    resources:
      requests:
        memory: "256Mi"
        cpu: "250m"
      limits:
        memory: "512Mi"
        cpu: "500m"

Example 4: Multi-container Django + Redis Pod
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: anihpj-redis
spec:
  containers:
  - name: django-app
    image: anihpj:v1.0

    - containerPort: 8000

Example 5: Django Pod with health checks
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: anihpj-health
spec:
  containers:
  - name: django-app
    image: anihpj:v1.0
    livenessProbe:
      httpGet:
        path: /jobpost/jobs/
        port: 8000
    readinessProbe:
      httpGet:
        path: /jobpost/skills/
        port: 8000
pod/anihpj-health created Health checks: Liveness ✓, Readiness ✓ Status: Running and Ready
Django QuerySet Output: >>> JobPost.objects.select_related('author', 'location').prefetch_related('skills') &lt;QuerySet [&lt;JobPost: Python Developer&gt;, &lt;JobPost: Frontend Developer&gt;, &lt;JobPost: Full Stack Developer&gt;, &lt;JobPost: Data Scientist&gt;, &lt;JobPost: UI/UX Designer&gt;]&gt;
Testing anihpj Django Views: # Test jobpost_list view curl http://anihpj-health:8000/jobpost/jobs/ # Returns: [{"title": "Python Developer", "description": "...", "skills": ["Python", "Django"]}, ...] # Test jobpost_detail view curl http://anihpj-health:8000/jobpost/jobs/1/ # Returns: {"title": "Python Developer", "description": "Full stack developer needed", "author": "ABC Corp"} # Test skills_list view curl http://anihpj-health:8000/jobpost/skills/ # Returns: [{"name": "Python"}, {"name": "JavaScript"}, {"name": "React"}]

PodSpec

Docs: Kubernetes PodSpec Documentation

Defines the desired state and configuration for a Pod running Django applications.

Full description

PodSpec is the specification section of a Pod resource that defines how your Django application should run. It includes container definitions, volumes, networking, scheduling constraints, and security contexts. The PodSpec is crucial for Django deployments as it controls resource allocation, environment variables, persistent storage for Django media files, and database connections. Understanding PodSpec is essential for optimizing Django application performance in Kubernetes.

Syntax

spec:
  containers:
  - name: container-name
    image: image:tag
    ports:
    - containerPort: 8000
  volumes:
  - name: volume-name
    persistentVolumeClaim:
      claimName: pvc-name
  restartPolicy: Always
  nodeSelector:
    disktype: ssd
  affinity:
    podAffinity:
      requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
      - labelSelector:
          matchLabels:
            app: database

Parameters description

  • containers: Array of container specifications running in the Pod. Each Django application typically needs one main container plus optional sidecars.
  • volumes: Array of volumes that containers can mount. Essential for Django static files, media uploads, and database persistence.
  • restartPolicy: Defines when containers should be restarted. Options: Always (default), OnFailure, Never. Use Always for Django web servers.
  • nodeSelector: Labels to constrain Pod scheduling to specific nodes. Useful for Django apps requiring SSD storage or GPU resources.
  • affinity: Advanced scheduling rules for Pod placement. Use to co-locate Django apps with databases or separate them for fault tolerance.
  • tolerations: Allows Pods to schedule on nodes with matching taints. Important for dedicated Django node pools.
  • securityContext: Pod-level security settings. Set user IDs, filesystem groups, and capabilities for Django containers.
  • serviceAccountName: Service account for Pod authentication. Required for Django apps accessing Kubernetes APIs or cloud resources.

Return value

PodSpec object: Configuration specification that Kubernetes uses to create and manage the Pod. Changes to PodSpec require Pod recreation.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Basic PodSpec
spec:
  containers:
  - name: anihpj-web
    image: anihpj:v1.0
Basic PodSpec created Container: anihpj-web Image: anihpj:v1.0
Django QuerySet Output: >>> JobPost.objects.all().count() 5 # Show actual job titles and salaries from demo data >>> list(JobPost.objects.values_list('title', 'salary')) [('Python Developer', 80000), ('Frontend Developer', 75000), ('Full Stack Developer', 85000), ('Data Scientist', 90000), ('UI/UX Designer', 80000)]
Example 2: PodSpec with volumes
spec:
  containers:
  - name: anihpj-app
    image: anihpj:v1.0
    volumeMounts:
    - name: media
      mountPath: /app/media
  volumes:
  - name: media
    emptyDir: {}
PodSpec with volume configured Volume: media mounted at /app/media Type: emptyDir
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Skills.objects.values_list('name', flat=True) &lt;QuerySet ['Python', 'JavaScript', 'React', 'Django', 'SQL']&gt;
Example 3: PodSpec with restart policy
spec:
  containers:
  - name: anihpj-worker
    image: anihpj-celery:v1.0
  restartPolicy: OnFailure
PodSpec with restart policy Policy: OnFailure Suitable for: Jobs and batch processing
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Author.objects.count() 5 # Show actual companies and designations from demo data >>> list(Author.objects.values_list('company', 'designation')) [('ABC Corp', 'Software Engineer'), ('XYZ Inc', 'Web Developer'), ('123 Industries', 'Full Stack Developer'), ('Tech Solutions', 'Data Scientist'), ('Tech Innovations', 'UI/UX Designer')]
Example 4: PodSpec with node selector
spec:
  containers:
  - name: anihpj-app
    image: anihpj:v1.0
  nodeSelector:
    disktype: ssd
PodSpec with node constraints Node selector: disktype=ssd Scheduling: SSD nodes only
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Location.objects.count() 5
Example 5: PodSpec with security context
spec:
  securityContext:
    runAsUser: 1000
  containers:
  - name: anihpj-secure
    image: anihpj:v1.0
PodSpec with security context User ID: 1000 Security: Non-root user
Django QuerySet Output: >>> JobPost.objects.filter(type='Full Time').count() 5 # Show actual job-skills relationships from demo data >>> JobPost.objects.get(title='Python Developer').skills.values_list('name', flat=True) &lt;QuerySet ['Python', 'Django']&gt; >>> JobPost.objects.get(title='Frontend Developer').skills.values_list('name', flat=True) &lt;QuerySet ['JavaScript', 'React']&gt; >>> JobPost.objects.get(title='Full Stack Developer').skills.values_list('name', flat=True) &lt;QuerySet ['Python', 'JavaScript', 'React', 'Django']&gt;

Containers

Array of container specifications that define the Django applications running in the Pod.

Full description

The containers field in PodSpec defines all containers that run within the Pod. For Django applications, this typically includes the main web server container and optional sidecar containers for caching, logging, or monitoring.

Syntax
spec:
  containers:
  - name: django-web
    image: jobpost:v1.0
    ports:
    - containerPort: 8000
Parameters description
  • name: Unique container name within the Pod
  • image: Docker image for the Django application
  • ports: Ports exposed by the container
Return value

Array of Container objects defining the Pod's containerized applications.

5 short examples with Django models
Example 1: Single Django container
containers:
- name: anihpj-web
  image: anihpj:v1.0
Single container configured Name: anihpj-web
>>> User.objects.count() 5
Example 2: Django with Redis sidecar
containers:
- name: anihpj-app
  image: anihpj:v1.0
- name: redis-cache
  image: redis:alpine
Multi-container Pod Containers: anihpj-app, redis-cache
>>> Skills.objects.filter(name__contains='Java').count() 1
Example 3: Django API container
containers:
- name: anihpj-api
  image: anihpj-api:v2.0
  ports:
  - containerPort: 8000
API container configured Port: 8000 exposed
>>> JobPost.objects.filter(salary__gte=80000).count() 4
Example 4: Django worker container
containers:
- name: anihpj-celery-worker
  image: anihpj-celery:v1.0
  command: ["celery", "worker"]
Worker container configured Command: celery worker
>>> Author.objects.filter(company__contains='Tech').count() 2
Example 5: Django with init container
containers:
- name: anihpj-app
  image: anihpj:v1.0
initContainers:
- name: anihpj-migrate
  image: anihpj:v1.0
  command: ["python", "manage.py", "migrate"]
Container with init configured Init: Database migration
>>> Location.objects.filter(country='USA').count() 4

Volumes

Storage volumes available to containers in the Pod for Django static files, media, and data persistence.

Full description

Volumes in PodSpec define storage that can be mounted by containers in the Pod. Essential for Django applications to store media files, static assets, database data, and configuration files that need to persist beyond container restarts.

Syntax
spec:
  volumes:
  - name: media-storage
    persistentVolumeClaim:
      claimName: django-media-pvc
  - name: static-files
    emptyDir: {}
Parameters description
  • name: Unique volume name referenced by containers
  • persistentVolumeClaim: Reference to persistent storage claim
  • emptyDir: Temporary directory shared between containers
  • configMap: Configuration data mounted as files
  • secret: Sensitive data mounted as files
Return value

Array of Volume objects defining storage available to Pod containers.

5 short examples with Django models
Example 1: Persistent volume for media
volumes:
- name: django-media
  persistentVolumeClaim:
    claimName: media-pvc
Persistent volume configured Name: django-media PVC: media-pvc
>>> JobPost.objects.exclude(description='').count() 5
Example 2: EmptyDir for temporary files
volumes:
- name: temp-storage
  emptyDir:
    sizeLimit: 1Gi
EmptyDir volume configured Size limit: 1Gi Type: Temporary storage
>>> Skills.objects.order_by('name').first().name 'Django'
Example 3: ConfigMap for Django settings
volumes:
- name: django-config
  configMap:
    name: django-settings
ConfigMap volume configured ConfigMap: django-settings Purpose: Configuration files
>>> Author.objects.values('designation').distinct().count() 5
Example 4: Secret for database credentials
volumes:
- name: db-credentials
  secret:
    secretName: postgres-secret
Secret volume configured Secret: postgres-secret Purpose: Database credentials
>>> Location.objects.values('city').distinct().count() 5
Example 5: HostPath for logs
volumes:
- name: log-storage
  hostPath:
    path: /var/log/django
    type: DirectoryOrCreate
HostPath volume configured Path: /var/log/django Type: DirectoryOrCreate
>>> JobPost.objects.latest('date').title 'UI/UX Designer'

Scheduling

Controls Pod placement on nodes using node selectors, affinity, and anti-affinity rules for Django applications.

Full description

Scheduling configuration in PodSpec determines where your Django application Pods are placed in the cluster. This includes node selection based on labels, Pod affinity for co-location with related services, and anti-affinity for fault tolerance and performance isolation.

Syntax
spec:
  nodeSelector:
    disktype: ssd
  affinity:
    nodeAffinity:
      requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
        nodeSelectorTerms:
        - matchExpressions:
          - key: zone
            operator: In
            values: ["us-west-1a"]
Parameters description
  • nodeSelector: Simple key-value labels for node selection
  • affinity.nodeAffinity: Advanced rules for node selection
  • affinity.podAffinity: Rules to co-locate with other Pods
  • affinity.podAntiAffinity: Rules to avoid co-location with other Pods
  • tolerations: Allows scheduling on tainted nodes
Return value

Scheduling constraints that influence Pod placement decisions by the Kubernetes scheduler.

5 short examples with Django models
Example 1: Node selector for SSD nodes
nodeSelector:
  disktype: ssd
  zone: us-west-1a
Node selector configured Disk: SSD required Zone: us-west-1a
>>> JobPost.objects.filter(author__company='ABC Corp').count() 1
Example 2: Pod affinity with database
affinity:
  podAffinity:
    preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
    - weight: 100
      podAffinityTerm:
        labelSelector:
          matchLabels:
            app: postgres
Pod affinity configured Target: postgres Pods Weight: 100 (preferred)
>>> Skills.objects.filter(jobpost__author__company='XYZ Inc').count() 2
Example 3: Anti-affinity for HA
affinity:
  podAntiAffinity:
    requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
    - labelSelector:
        matchLabels:
          app: anihpj
      topologyKey: kubernetes.io/hostname
Anti-affinity configured Rule: No two anihpj Pods on same node High availability: Enabled
>>> Author.objects.filter(jobpost__type='Part Time').count() 0
Example 4: Tolerations for dedicated nodes
tolerations:
- key: "dedicated"
  operator: "Equal"
  value: "django"
  effect: "NoSchedule"
Toleration configured Key: dedicated=django Effect: NoSchedule tolerated
>>> Location.objects.filter(state__in=['CA', 'NY']).count() 3
Example 5: Complex affinity rules
affinity:
  nodeAffinity:
    requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
      nodeSelectorTerms:
      - matchExpressions:
        - key: node-role
          operator: In
          values: ["worker", "compute"]
Node affinity configured Allowed nodes: worker, compute Rule: Required
>>> JobPost.objects.aggregate(avg_salary=Avg('salary'))['avg_salary'] 82000.0

Lifecycle

Defines lifecycle hooks and restart behavior for Django containers in the Pod.

Full description

Lifecycle configuration in PodSpec controls container restart policies and lifecycle hooks. For Django applications, this determines how containers behave during startup, shutdown, and failure scenarios. Essential for graceful Django application lifecycle management.

Syntax
spec:
  restartPolicy: Always
  containers:
  - name: django-app
    image: jobpost:v1.0
    lifecycle:
      postStart:
        exec:
          command: ["/bin/sh", "-c", "echo Container started"]
      preStop:
        exec:
          command: ["/bin/sh", "-c", "echo Container stopping"]
Parameters description
  • restartPolicy: When to restart containers - Always, OnFailure, Never
  • lifecycle.postStart: Hook executed after container starts
  • lifecycle.preStop: Hook executed before container stops
Return value

Lifecycle configuration that controls Pod and container behavior during state transitions.

Security context

Defines security settings and constraints for Django containers in the Pod.

Full description

Security context in PodSpec sets Pod-level security constraints for Django applications. This includes user IDs, group IDs, filesystem permissions, capabilities, and security profiles that enhance container security and compliance.

Syntax
spec:
  securityContext:
    runAsNonRoot: true
    runAsUser: 1000
    runAsGroup: 3000
    fsGroup: 2000
    supplementalGroups: [1000, 2000]
Parameters description
  • runAsNonRoot: Require containers to run as non-root user
  • runAsUser: User ID to run containers
  • runAsGroup: Primary group ID for containers
  • fsGroup: Filesystem group ownership for volumes
  • supplementalGroups: Additional group memberships
Return value

Security context configuration applied to all containers in the Pod.

  runAsNonRoot: true
  runAsUser: 1000
  runAsGroup: 3000
  fsGroup: 2000
Security: Complete context User: 1000, Group: 3000, FS: 2000

Container

Docs: Kubernetes Container Documentation

Defines a single container within a Pod, specifying the Django application image, resources, and runtime configuration.

Full description

Container specification defines how your Django application runs within a Pod. It includes the Docker image, resource requirements, environment variables, volume mounts, ports, and health checks. For Django applications, container configuration is critical for performance, security, and reliability. You'll typically define main containers for Django web servers, worker processes, or management commands, plus optional sidecar containers for logging, monitoring, or caching.

Syntax

containers:
- name: container-name
  image: image:tag
  ports:
  - containerPort: 8000
    name: http
  env:
  - name: DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE
    value: "myproject.settings.production"
  resources:
    requests:
      memory: "512Mi"
      cpu: "500m"
    limits:
      memory: "1Gi"
      cpu: "1000m"
  volumeMounts:
  - name: static-files
    mountPath: /app/static
  livenessProbe:
    httpGet:
      path: /health/
      port: 8000
  readinessProbe:
    httpGet:
      path: /ready/
      port: 8000

Parameters description

  • name: Unique name for the container within the Pod. Use descriptive names like "django-web", "celery-worker", or "django-migrate".
  • image: Docker image to run. Always use specific tags for production Django deployments (e.g., "myapp:v2.1.3").
  • ports: Array of ports the container exposes. Django web servers typically expose port 8000 or 80.
  • env: Environment variables passed to the container. Include Django settings module, database URLs, secret keys, and feature flags.
  • resources.requests: Minimum CPU and memory guaranteed to the container. Essential for Django application performance and scheduling.
  • resources.limits: Maximum CPU and memory the container can use. Prevents Django apps from consuming excessive cluster resources.
  • volumeMounts: Directories where volumes are mounted in the container. Use for Django static files, media uploads, and configuration.
  • livenessProbe: Health check to determine if the container is running. Kubernetes restarts failed containers.
  • readinessProbe: Health check to determine if the container is ready to receive traffic. Important for Django startup sequences.

Return value

Container object: Runtime container specification used by Kubernetes to create and manage the container process within the Pod.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Basic Django container
containers:
- name: anihpj-web
  image: anihpj:v1.0
  ports:
  - containerPort: 8000
Container: anihpj-web Image: anihpj:v1.0 Port: 8000
Django QuerySet Output: >>> JobPost.objects.count() 5
Example 2: Django with environment variables
containers:
- name: django-app
  image: jobpost:v1.0
  env:
  - name: DEBUG
    value: "False"
  - name: DATABASE_URL
    value: "postgres://user:pass@db:5432/anihpj"
Container with environment configured DEBUG: False Database: PostgreSQL connection
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Location.objects.filter(country='USA').count() 4
Example 3: Django with resource limits
containers:
- name: django-api
  image: jobpost-api:v1.0
  resources:
    requests:
      memory: "256Mi"
      cpu: "250m"
    limits:
      memory: "512Mi"
      cpu: "500m"
Container with resource management Requests: 256Mi memory, 250m CPU Limits: 512Mi memory, 500m CPU
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Skills.objects.all().count() 5
Example 4: Django with health checks
containers:
- name: django-health
  image: jobpost:v1.0
  livenessProbe:
    httpGet:
      path: /health/
      port: 8000
    initialDelaySeconds: 30
  readinessProbe:
    httpGet:
      path: /ready/
      port: 8000
    initialDelaySeconds: 5
Container with health monitoring Liveness: /health/ (30s delay) Readiness: /ready/ (5s delay)
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Author.objects.count() 5
Example 5: Django with volume mounts
containers:
- name: django-files
  image: jobpost:v1.0
  volumeMounts:
  - name: media-storage
    mountPath: /app/media
  - name: static-files
    mountPath: /app/static
Container with persistent storage Media: /app/media Static: /app/static
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Location.objects.count() 5

Image

Specifies the Docker image to run for the Django application container.

Full description

The image field specifies which Docker image to run in the container. For Django applications, this includes your application code, Python runtime, dependencies, and configuration. Always use specific tags in production to ensure consistent deployments and easy rollbacks.

Syntax

- name: django-app
  image: registry.example.com/jobpost:v2.1.0
  imagePullPolicy: Always
Parameters description
  • image: Full image name including registry, repository, and tag
  • imagePullPolicy: When to pull the image - Always, IfNotPresent, Never
Return value

Image configuration that determines which Docker image Kubernetes runs for the container.

Environment variables

Sets environment variables for Django application configuration and secrets.

Full description

Environment variables provide runtime configuration to Django containers. This includes Django settings, database connections, API keys, feature flags, and other configuration that shouldn't be hardcoded in the image. Essential for twelve-factor app methodology and environment-specific deployments.

Syntax
containers:
- name: django-app
  env:
  - name: DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE
    value: "anihpj.settings.production"
  - name: DATABASE_URL
    valueFrom:
      secretKeyRef:
        name: db-secret
        key: url
  - name: POD_NAME
    valueFrom:
      fieldRef:
        fieldPath: metadata.name
Parameters description
  • name: Environment variable name available in the container
  • value: Direct string value for the environment variable
  • valueFrom.secretKeyRef: Reference to a value stored in a Kubernetes Secret
  • valueFrom.configMapKeyRef: Reference to a value stored in a ConfigMap
  • valueFrom.fieldRef: Reference to a Pod or container field value
Return value

Environment variable configuration passed to the container at runtime.

5 short examples with Django models
Example 1: Django settings module
env:
- name: DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE
  value: "anihpj.settings.production"

      name: postgres-secret
      key: database-url

>>> Skills.objects.exclude(name='').count() 5
Example 3: Debug mode from ConfigMap
env:
- name: DEBUG
  valueFrom:
    configMapKeyRef:
      name: django-config
      key: debug-mode
Environment: DEBUG Source: ConfigMap (django-config) Configuration: Centralized settings
>>> Author.objects.filter(designation__icontains='Developer').count() 3
Example 4: Pod metadata injection

- name: POD_NAME
  valueFrom:
    fieldRef:
      fieldPath: metadata.name
- name: NODE_NAME
  valueFrom:
    fieldRef:
      fieldPath: spec.nodeName
Environment: POD_NAME, NODE_NAME Source: Pod metadata fields Purpose: Runtime identification

Example 5: Multiple environment variables

- name: REDIS_URL
  value: "redis://redis-service:6379/0"
- name: CELERY_BROKER_URL
  value: "redis://redis-service:6379/1"
- name: ALLOWED_HOSTS
  value: "jobpost.example.com,www.jobpost.com"

Volumes

Defines volume mounts for persistent storage in Django containers.

Full description

Volume mounts in containers specify where Pod volumes are mounted within the container filesystem. For Django applications, this is essential for persistent storage of media files, static assets, database files, logs, and configuration. Volume mounts enable data persistence across container restarts.

Syntax
containers:
- name: django-app
  volumeMounts:
  - name: media-storage
    mountPath: /app/media
    readOnly: false
  - name: static-files
    mountPath: /app/static
    readOnly: true
Parameters description
  • name: Name of the volume defined in the Pod spec
  • mountPath: Path within the container where the volume is mounted
  • readOnly: Whether the volume is mounted read-only
  • subPath: Path within the volume from which to mount
Return value

Volume mount configuration that makes Pod volumes available within the container.

5 short examples with Django models

>>> Skills.objects.order_by('name').values_list('name', flat=True).first() 'Django'
Example 3: Configuration from ConfigMap
volumeMounts:
- name: django-config
  mountPath: /app/config
  readOnly: true
Volume: django-config Mount: /app/config (read-only) Source: ConfigMap
>>> Author.objects.values('designation').distinct().count() 5
Example 4: Database volume with subPath
volumeMounts:
- name: db-storage
  mountPath: /var/lib/postgresql/data
  subPath: postgres
Volume: db-storage Mount: /var/lib/postgresql/data SubPath: postgres directory
>>> Location.objects.exclude(zip='').count() 4
Example 5: Multiple volume mounts

- name: media
  mountPath: /app/media

  mountPath: /app/logs

  mountPath: /tmp/django-cache

Resources

Specifies CPU and memory resource requests and limits for Django containers.

Full description

Resource configuration defines CPU and memory requirements for Django containers. Requests guarantee minimum resources for scheduling and performance, while limits prevent containers from consuming excessive cluster resources. Proper resource management is crucial for Django application stability and cluster efficiency.

Syntax
containers:
- name: django-app
  resources:
    requests:
      memory: "512Mi"
      cpu: "500m"
    limits:
      memory: "1Gi"
      cpu: "1000m"
Parameters description
  • requests.memory: Minimum memory guaranteed to the container (e.g., "512Mi", "1Gi")
  • requests.cpu: Minimum CPU guaranteed to the container (e.g., "500m", "0.5")
  • limits.memory: Maximum memory the container can use before being killed
  • limits.cpu: Maximum CPU the container can use (throttled beyond this)
Return value

Resource configuration that determines container scheduling and runtime resource allocation.

5 short examples with Django models
Example 1: Basic Django web server
resources:
  requests:
    memory: "256Mi"
    cpu: "250m"
  limits:
    memory: "512Mi"
    cpu: "500m"
Resources: Web server optimized Requests: 256Mi memory, 250m CPU Limits: 512Mi memory, 500m CPU
>>> JobPost.objects.filter(salary__range=(70000, 90000)).count() 4
Example 2: Memory-intensive Django app
resources:
  requests:

Lifecycle

Defines lifecycle hooks for Django container startup and shutdown events.

Full description

Container lifecycle hooks allow execution of commands during specific container events. For Django applications, this enables custom initialization, graceful shutdowns, cleanup tasks, and integration with external services during container startup and termination phases.

Syntax
containers:
- name: django-app
  lifecycle:
    postStart:
      exec:
        command: ["python", "manage.py", "collectstatic", "--noinput"]
    preStop:
      exec:
        command: ["/bin/sh", "-c", "sleep 15"]
Parameters description
  • postStart: Hook executed immediately after container creation
  • preStop: Hook executed immediately before container termination
  • exec.command: Command to execute for the lifecycle hook
  • httpGet: Alternative HTTP request-based hook
Return value

Lifecycle configuration that defines custom behavior during container state transitions.

5 short examples with Django models
Example 1: Django static files collection
lifecycle:
  postStart:
    exec:
      command: ["python", "manage.py", "collectstatic", "--noinput"]
PostStart: Collect static files Command: python manage.py collectstatic Purpose: Prepare static assets
>>> JobPost.objects.exclude(slug__isnull=True).count() 5
Example 2: Graceful shutdown delay
lifecycle:
  preStop:
    exec:
      command: ["/bin/sh", "-c", "sleep 30"]
PreStop: Graceful shutdown Command: sleep 30 Purpose: Allow request completion
>>> Skills.objects.filter(jobpost__type='Full Time').distinct().count() 5
Example 3: Database migration on startup
lifecycle:
  postStart:
    exec:
      command: ["python", "manage.py", "migrate", "--noinput"]
PostStart: Database migration Command: python manage.py migrate Purpose: Update database schema
>>> Author.objects.select_related('user').count() 5
Example 4: Cache warming
lifecycle:
  postStart:
    exec:
      command: ["python", "manage.py", "warm_cache"]
PostStart: Warm cache Command: python manage.py warm_cache Purpose: Preload frequently accessed data
>>> Location.objects.values('country').distinct().count() 2
Example 5: HTTP-based lifecycle hook
lifecycle:
  postStart:
    httpGet:
      path: /api/startup/
      port: 8000
PostStart: HTTP hook URL: /api/startup/ on port 8000 Purpose: Custom startup logic
>>> User.objects.filter(date_joined__year=2024).count() 5

Security Context

Defines security settings and constraints for individual Django containers.

Full description

Container-level security context overrides Pod-level security settings for specific containers. This includes user IDs, capabilities, security profiles, and filesystem permissions. Essential for Django applications requiring specific security constraints or privilege levels.

Syntax
containers:
- name: django-app
  securityContext:
    runAsNonRoot: true
    runAsUser: 1000
    runAsGroup: 3000
    allowPrivilegeEscalation: false
    readOnlyRootFilesystem: true
    capabilities:
      drop:
      - ALL
      add:
      - NET_BIND_SERVICE
Parameters description
  • runAsNonRoot: Require container to run as non-root user
  • runAsUser: User ID to run the container process
  • runAsGroup: Primary group ID for the container process
  • allowPrivilegeEscalation: Whether process can gain more privileges than parent
  • readOnlyRootFilesystem: Whether container's root filesystem is read-only
  • capabilities: Linux capabilities to add or drop
Return value

Security context configuration applied specifically to this container.

5 short examples with Django models
Example 1: Non-root Django container
securityContext:
  runAsNonRoot: true
  runAsUser: 1000
Security: Non-root execution User ID: 1000 Enhanced security for Django app
>>> JobPost.objects.filter(author__company='ABC Corp').count() 1
Example 2: Read-only filesystem
securityContext:
  readOnlyRootFilesystem: true
  runAsUser: 1001
Security: Read-only root filesystem User: 1001 Immutable container filesystem
>>> Skills.objects.exclude(name__exact='').count() 5
Example 3: Dropped capabilities
securityContext:
  capabilities:
    drop:
    - ALL
    add:
    - NET_BIND_SERVICE
Security: Minimal capabilities Dropped: ALL capabilities Added: NET_BIND_SERVICE only
>>> Author.objects.filter(company__contains='Corp').count() 1
Example 4: No privilege escalation
securityContext:
  allowPrivilegeEscalation: false
  runAsUser: 1000
  runAsGroup: 1000
Security: No privilege escalation User: 1000, Group: 1000 Process isolation enhanced
>>> Location.objects.filter(state__len=2).count() 4
Example 5: Complete security hardening
securityContext:
  runAsNonRoot: true
  runAsUser: 1000
  readOnlyRootFilesystem: true
  allowPrivilegeEscalation: false
Security: Full hardening Non-root: ✓, Read-only: ✓ No escalation: ✓
>>> User.objects.filter(username__len__gte=4).count() 5

EphemeralContainer

Docs: Kubernetes EphemeralContainer Documentation

Temporary containers added to running Pods for debugging and troubleshooting Django applications.

Full description

EphemeralContainers are temporary containers that can be added to already running Pods for debugging purposes. Unlike regular containers, ephemeral containers don't restart and are removed when the Pod terminates. For Django applications, they're invaluable for troubleshooting production issues, inspecting running processes, and accessing Django shells without disrupting the main application.

Syntax

ephemeralContainers:
- name: debugger
  image: jobpost-debug:latest
  command: ["sh"]
  stdin: true
  tty: true
  targetContainerName: django-web

Parameters description

  • name: Unique name for the ephemeral container within the Pod
  • image: Docker image containing debugging tools and utilities
  • command: Command to run in the ephemeral container
  • stdin: Whether to allocate stdin to the container
  • tty: Whether to allocate a TTY for the container
  • targetContainerName: Name of the target container to debug

Return value

EphemeralContainer object: Temporary container configuration added to the running Pod for debugging purposes.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Basic debug container
ephemeralContainers:
- name: debug
  image: busybox
  command: ["sh"]
  stdin: true
  tty: true
Ephemeral container: debug Image: busybox Interactive: Shell with stdin/tty
Django QuerySet Output: >>> JobPost.objects.count() 5
Example 2: Django shell access
ephemeralContainers:
- name: django-shell
  image: jobpost:debug
  command: ["python", "manage.py", "shell"]
  stdin: true
  tty: true
Ephemeral container: django-shell Command: Django management shell Interactive: Direct model access
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Skills.objects.filter(name__icontains='Script').count() 1
Example 3: Network debugging
ephemeralContainers:
- name: netdebug
  image: nicolaka/netshoot
  command: ["bash"]
  stdin: true
  tty: true
Ephemeral container: netdebug Image: nicolaka/netshoot Purpose: Network troubleshooting
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Skills.objects.all().count() 5
Example 4: File system inspection
ephemeralContainers:
- name: file-inspector
  image: alpine
  command: ["sh"]
  stdin: true
  tty: true
  volumeMounts:
  - name: app-storage
    mountPath: /debug-mount
Ephemeral container: file-inspector Mount: app-storage at /debug-mount Purpose: File system debugging
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Author.objects.count() 5
Example 5: Process debugging
ephemeralContainers:
- name: process-debug
  image: jobpost:debug
  command: ["bash"]
  stdin: true
  tty: true
  targetContainerName: django-web
Ephemeral container: process-debug Target: django-web container Purpose: Process inspection and debugging
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Location.objects.count() 5

Not allowed

Restrictions and limitations for ephemeral containers.

Full description

Ephemeral containers have specific limitations and restrictions. They cannot be used for production workloads, don't support certain fields, and have limited lifecycle management. Understanding these restrictions is important for proper Django application debugging.

Syntax
# These fields are NOT allowed in ephemeral containers:
# - ports (no port exposure)
# - livenessProbe (no health checks)  
# - readinessProbe (no readiness checks)
# - resources.requests (only limits allowed)
# - lifecycle (limited lifecycle support)
Parameters description
  • ports: Cannot expose ports in ephemeral containers
  • probes: No liveness or readiness probes allowed
  • resource requests: Only resource limits are supported
  • restart policy: Cannot specify restart behavior
Return value

Error messages for unsupported fields in ephemeral container specifications.

5 short examples with Django models
Example 1: Invalid port configuration
# ERROR: ports not allowed
ports:
- containerPort: 8000
ERROR: EphemeralContainer cannot expose ports Fix: Remove ports field
>>> JobPost.objects.count() # Still accessible via debugging 5
Example 2: Invalid health probes
# ERROR: probes not allowed
livenessProbe:
  exec:
    command: ["echo", "alive"]
ERROR: EphemeralContainer cannot have probes Fix: Remove liveness/readiness probes
>>> Skills.objects.all().count() 5
Example 3: Invalid resource requests
# ERROR: requests not allowed, only limits
resources:
  requests:
    memory: "256Mi"
ERROR: EphemeralContainer cannot request resources Fix: Use only resource limits
>>> Author.objects.count() 5
Example 4: Correct ephemeral configuration
# CORRECT: Only allowed fields
name: debugger
image: busybox
command: ["sh"]
stdin: true
tty: true
SUCCESS: Valid ephemeral container Configuration: Basic debugging setup
>>> Location.objects.count() 5
Example 5: Temporary nature
# Ephemeral containers:
# - Don't restart on failure
# - Are removed when Pod terminates
# - Cannot be updated after creation
LIMITATION: Temporary debugging only Lifecycle: No restart, removed with Pod
>>> User.objects.count() 5

LifecycleHandler

Docs: Kubernetes LifecycleHandler Documentation

Defines actions to be taken during container lifecycle events for Django applications.

Full description

LifecycleHandler specifies actions to execute during container lifecycle events like startup and shutdown. For Django applications, this enables custom initialization, graceful shutdowns, cleanup tasks, database migrations, static file collection, and integration with external services during container lifecycle transitions.

Syntax

lifecycle:
  postStart:
    exec:
      command: ["python", "manage.py", "collectstatic", "--noinput"]
  preStop:
    httpGet:
      path: /api/shutdown/
      port: 8000
    exec:
      command: ["/bin/sh", "-c", "sleep 30"]

Parameters description

  • exec: Execute a command inside the container during lifecycle event
  • httpGet: Send HTTP GET request to specified path and port
  • tcpSocket: Perform TCP socket check on specified port

Return value

LifecycleHandler configuration that defines custom actions during container state transitions.

NodeAffinity

Docs: Kubernetes NodeAffinity Documentation

Controls Pod placement on nodes based on node properties for Django applications.

Full description

NodeAffinity defines rules for scheduling Django Pods on specific nodes based on node labels and properties. This enables deployment of Django applications on nodes with specific characteristics like SSD storage, GPU capabilities, geographic location, or instance types optimized for Django workloads.

Syntax

affinity:
  nodeAffinity:
    requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
      nodeSelectorTerms:
      - matchExpressions:
        - key: disktype
          operator: In
          values: ["ssd"]
    preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
    - weight: 100
      preference:
        matchExpressions:
        - key: zone
          operator: In
          values: ["us-west-1a"]

Parameters description

  • requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution: Hard constraints that must be satisfied for Pod scheduling
  • preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution: Soft preferences with weights for Pod scheduling
  • nodeSelectorTerms: Node selection criteria based on node labels
  • matchExpressions: Label selector expressions with key, operator, and values
  • weight: Priority weight for preferred scheduling rules (1-100)

Return value

NodeAffinity configuration that influences Pod placement decisions based on node characteristics.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: SSD storage requirement
nodeAffinity:
  requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
    nodeSelectorTerms:
    - matchExpressions:
      - key: disktype
        operator: In
        values: ["ssd"]
NodeAffinity: SSD storage required Rule: Hard constraint for disk type Performance: Optimized for Django database
Django QuerySet Output: >>> JobPost.objects.filter(salary__gte=80000).count() 4
Example 2: Zone preference
nodeAffinity:
  preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
  - weight: 80
    preference:
      matchExpressions:
      - key: topology.kubernetes.io/zone
        operator: In
        values: ["us-west-1a", "us-west-1b"]
NodeAffinity: Zone preference Weight: 80 for us-west zones Availability: Multi-zone deployment
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Skills.objects.filter(name__in=['Python', 'Django']).count() 2
Example 3: Instance type requirement
nodeAffinity:
  requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
    nodeSelectorTerms:
    - matchExpressions:
      - key: node.kubernetes.io/instance-type
        operator: In
        values: ["c5.large", "c5.xlarge"]
NodeAffinity: Compute-optimized instances Types: c5.large, c5.xlarge Performance: High CPU for Django processing
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Author.objects.filter(jobpost__salary__gt=75000).distinct().count() 5
Example 4: Environment-based scheduling
nodeAffinity:
  requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
    nodeSelectorTerms:
    - matchExpressions:
      - key: environment
        operator: In
        values: ["production"]
      - key: workload
        operator: In
        values: ["django", "web"]
NodeAffinity: Production Django nodes Environment: production Workload: django, web
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Location.objects.values('country').distinct().count() 2
Example 5: Combined requirements and preferences
nodeAffinity:
  requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
    nodeSelectorTerms:
    - matchExpressions:
      - key: kubernetes.io/os
        operator: In
        values: ["linux"]
  preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
  - weight: 50
    preference:
      matchExpressions:
      - key: node-role
        operator: In
        values: ["django-worker"]
NodeAffinity: Linux required, Django worker preferred OS: linux (required) Role: django-worker (weight 50)
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Location.objects.filter(zip__startswith='9').count() 2
env: - name: DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE value: "myproject.settings.production" resources: requests: memory: "512Mi" cpu: "500m" limits: memory: "1Gi" cpu: "1000m" volumeMounts: - name: static-files mountPath: /app/static livenessProbe: httpGet: path: /health/ port: 8000 readinessProbe: httpGet: path: /ready/ port: 8000

Parameters description

  • name: Unique name for the container within the Pod. Use descriptive names like "django-web", "celery-worker", or "django-migrate".
  • image: Docker image to run. Always use specific tags for production Django deployments (e.g., "myapp:v2.1.3").
  • ports: Array of ports the container exposes. Django web servers typically expose port 8000 or 80.
  • env: Environment variables passed to the container. Include Django settings module, database URLs, secret keys, and feature flags.
  • resources.requests: Minimum CPU and memory guaranteed to the container. Essential for Django application performance and scheduling.
  • resources.limits: Maximum CPU and memory the container can use. Prevents Django apps from consuming excessive cluster resources.
  • volumeMounts: Directories where volumes are mounted in the container. Use for Django static files, media uploads, and configuration.
  • livenessProbe: Health check to determine if the container is running. Kubernetes restarts failed containers.
  • readinessProbe: Health check to determine if the container is ready to receive traffic. Important for Django startup sequences.

Return value

Container object: Runtime container specification used by Kubernetes to create and manage the container process within the Pod.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Django web server container
containers:

  - containerPort: 8000
    name: http
  env:
  - name: DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE
    value: "anihpj.settings.production"

    limits:
      memory: "512Mi"
      cpu: "500m"
Container: django-api Memory: 256Mi request, 512Mi limit CPU: 250m request, 500m limit Resource management: Optimized

  image: jobpost:v1.0
  volumeMounts:
  - name: media-storage
    mountPath: /app/media
  - name: static-files
    mountPath: /app/staticfiles
Container: django-files Volume mounts: /app/media, /app/staticfiles Storage: Persistent for user uploads
Django QuerySet Output: >>> Author.objects.values('company').distinct().count() 5
Example 4: Django container with health probes

      path: /ready/
      port: 8000
    initialDelaySeconds: 5
    periodSeconds: 5

Django QuerySet Output: >>> JobPost.objects.filter(salary__range=(70000, 90000)).count() 4
Example 5: Django Celery worker container

  image: jobpost-celery:v1.0

  args: ["-A", "anihpj", "worker", "--loglevel=info"]
  env:
  - name: CELERY_BROKER_URL
    value: "redis://redis-svc:6379/0"

Django QuerySet Output: >>> Location.objects.filter(city__in=['New York', 'Los Angeles']).count() 2

Pod Operations

Docs: Kubernetes Pod Operations

Complete CRUD operations for managing Django application Pods in Kubernetes.

Full description

Pod operations provide comprehensive management capabilities for your Django applications running in Kubernetes. These operations include creating new Pods, reading Pod status and logs, updating Pod configurations, and deleting Pods when no longer needed. Understanding Pod operations is essential for deploying, monitoring, and troubleshooting Django applications in production Kubernetes environments.

Available Operations

  • create: Create a new Pod running your Django application
  • get: Read the current state and configuration of a Pod
  • list: List all Pods, optionally filtered by labels or namespace
  • update: Replace the entire Pod configuration (requires Pod recreation)
  • patch: Partially update Pod metadata or status
  • delete: Remove a Pod and all its containers
  • logs: Retrieve container logs from Django applications
  • exec: Execute commands inside running Django containers

Return value

Varies by operation: Pod objects, lists, status codes, or log streams depending on the specific operation performed.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Create Django Pod
# Create Pod from YAML file
kubectl create -f jobpost-web-pod.yaml

# Create Pod directly with kubectl
kubectl run jobpost-web --image=jobpost:v1.0 --port=8000

# Get detailed Pod information

# Get Pod YAML configuration

# List all Pods
kubectl get pods

# List Pods with specific labels
kubectl get pods -l app=jobpost

# List Pods in all namespaces
kubectl get pods --all-namespaces

Example 4: View Pod logs

Binding

Docs: Kubernetes Binding v1

Binding ties one object to another; for example, a pod is bound to a node by a scheduler.

📋 Binding Components Overview

Binding ties one object to another; for example, a pod is bound to a node by a scheduler. This is a fundamental process in Kubernetes that determines where your Django applications will run in the cluster.

For Django applications, Bindings are created automatically by the Kubernetes scheduler when your pods need to be placed on specific nodes. The scheduler analyzes resource requirements, node capacity, taints and tolerations, and affinity rules to determine the optimal placement for your Django application pods.

💡 Note: Bindings are typically created automatically by the Kubernetes scheduler. Django developers rarely need to create Bindings manually, but understanding them helps with troubleshooting scheduling issues and optimizing pod placement.

🔧 Core Components

Binding Object Creates relationships between Kubernetes objects, typically Pod-to-Node

🔧 Binding Operations Overview

📋 Operation Categories

Create Operations Create Pod-to-Node bindings with POST requests
Read Operations Query binding information with GET requests
Scheduler Operations Automatic binding creation by Kubernetes scheduler
All Detailed Overviews Jump to complete component specifications

Binding Overview

Docs: Kubernetes Binding Resource

Binding establishes a relationship between Kubernetes objects, most commonly used by the scheduler to bind Pods to Nodes.

Full description

📋 Binding Fields Overview

apiVersion API version (v1) for Binding resources
kind Resource type identifier (Binding)
metadata Standard metadata with name, namespace, and labels
target Reference to target object (typically Node for Pod bindings)
target.apiVersion API version of target object (usually v1 for Nodes)
target.kind Type of target object (commonly Node)
target.name Name of specific target object (Node name)

Binding is a Kubernetes API resource that creates a relationship between two objects. The most common use case is when the Kubernetes scheduler creates a Binding to tie a Pod to a specific Node. This process is fundamental to how Django applications get scheduled and placed on worker nodes in your cluster. When you deploy a Django application, the scheduler evaluates resource requirements, node capacity, and scheduling constraints to determine the best node for your Pod, then creates a Binding to make this assignment official.

Syntax

apiVersion: v1
kind: Binding
metadata:
  name: anihpj-web-binding
  namespace: default
target:
  apiVersion: v1
  kind: Node
  name: worker-node-1

Parameters description

  • apiVersion: Always "v1" for Binding resources. This specifies the API version used for the Binding object.
  • kind: Always "Binding" to identify this as a Binding resource type.
  • metadata: Standard object metadata including name, namespace, labels, and annotations for the Binding.
  • target: Reference to the target object that the source object will be bound to (typically a Node for Pod bindings).
  • target.apiVersion: API version of the target object (usually "v1" for Nodes).
  • target.kind: Type of the target object (commonly "Node" for Pod-to-Node bindings).
  • target.name: Name of the specific target object (the Node name where the Pod will run).

Return value

Binding object: Establishes the relationship between source and target objects, confirming the scheduling decision for Django Pods.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Django web server binding
apiVersion: v1
kind: Binding
metadata:
  name: jobpost-web-binding
  namespace: default
target:
  apiVersion: v1
  kind: Node
  name: worker-node-1
Binding: jobpost-web bound to worker-node-1 Target: Node worker-node-1 Status: Pod scheduled successfully
Example 2: Django API server binding
apiVersion: v1
kind: Binding
metadata:
  name: anihpj-api-binding
  namespace: production
target:
  apiVersion: v1
  kind: Node
  name: high-memory-node-2
Binding: anihpj-api bound to high-memory-node-2 Namespace: production Node: Optimized for Django API workloads
Example 3: Django Celery worker binding
apiVersion: v1
kind: Binding
metadata:
  name: celery-worker-binding
  namespace: default
target:
  apiVersion: v1
  kind: Node
  name: cpu-optimized-node-3
Binding: celery-worker bound to cpu-optimized-node-3 Purpose: Background task processing Node: CPU-optimized for Django tasks
Example 4: Django database migration binding
apiVersion: v1
kind: Binding
metadata:
  name: django-migrate-binding
  namespace: staging
target:
  apiVersion: v1
  kind: Node
  name: ssd-storage-node-4
Binding: django-migrate bound to ssd-storage-node-4 Environment: staging Storage: SSD for fast database operations
Example 5: Django Redis cache binding
apiVersion: v1
kind: Binding
metadata:
  name: redis-cache-binding
  namespace: default
target:
  apiVersion: v1
  kind: Node
  name: memory-optimized-node-5
Binding: redis-cache bound to memory-optimized-node-5 Service: Redis for Django caching Node: Memory-optimized for cache performance

Binding Operations

Docs: Kubernetes Binding Operations

Operations for creating Binding resources to establish relationships between Kubernetes objects in Django deployments.

Full description

Binding operations provide the API endpoints for creating relationships between Kubernetes objects. These operations are primarily used by the Kubernetes scheduler to bind Pods to Nodes, but can also be used programmatically when you need custom scheduling logic for your Django applications. Understanding these operations is essential for advanced deployment scenarios where you need fine-grained control over Pod placement in your Django infrastructure.

create create a Binding

HTTP Request: POST /api/v1/namespaces/{namespace}/bindings

Creates a new Binding resource in the specified namespace to establish object relationships.

Full description

This operation creates a new Binding resource that establishes a relationship between two Kubernetes objects. Most commonly used by the scheduler to bind Pods to Nodes, but can be used for custom scheduling scenarios in Django deployments. When creating a Binding, you specify the target object (usually a Node) that the source object should be bound to.

Syntax
POST /api/v1/namespaces/{namespace}/bindings
Content-Type: application/json

{
  "apiVersion": "v1",
  "kind": "Binding",
  "metadata": {
    "name": "binding-name",
    "namespace": "namespace"
  },
  "target": {
    "apiVersion": "v1",
    "kind": "Node",
    "name": "target-node"
  }
}
Parameters description
  • namespace (path): Target namespace where the Binding will be created. Use "default" for development or specific environment namespaces like "production".
  • body (required): Complete Binding resource specification including metadata and target object reference.
  • dryRun (query): When set to "All", validates the request without creating the actual Binding resource.
  • fieldManager (query): Name of the manager responsible for this Binding operation, useful for tracking ownership.
  • fieldValidation (query): Validation level for the request fields ("Ignore", "Warn", or "Strict").
  • pretty (query): When set to "true", formats the API response for human readability.
Return value

HTTP Status Codes: 200 (OK), 201 (Created), 202 (Accepted), or 401 (Unauthorized) with the created Binding object.

5 short examples with Django models
Example 1: Create web server binding
curl -X POST /api/v1/namespaces/default/bindings \
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d '{
    "apiVersion": "v1",
    "kind": "Binding",
    "metadata": {"name": "jobpost-web-binding"},
    "target": {"kind": "Node", "name": "web-node-1"}
  }'
Status: 201 Created Binding: jobpost-web-binding created Target: web-node-1
Example 2: Create API binding with validation
curl -X POST /api/v1/namespaces/production/bindings?dryRun=All \
  -d '{
    "apiVersion": "v1",
    "kind": "Binding",
    "metadata": {"name": "api-binding"},
    "target": {"kind": "Node", "name": "api-node-2"}
  }'
Status: 200 OK (dry run) Validation: Passed Target: api-node-2 (validated)
Example 3: Create Celery worker binding
curl -X POST /api/v1/namespaces/default/bindings \
  -d '{
    "apiVersion": "v1",
    "kind": "Binding",
    "metadata": {"name": "celery-binding"},
    "target": {"kind": "Node", "name": "worker-node-3"}
  }'
Status: 201 Created Binding: celery-binding created Purpose: Background task processing
Example 4: Create database binding
curl -X POST /api/v1/namespaces/staging/bindings \
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d '{
    "apiVersion": "v1",
    "kind": "Binding",
    "metadata": {"name": "db-binding"},
    "target": {"kind": "Node", "name": "db-node-4"}
  }'
Status: 201 Created Binding: db-binding created Environment: staging database
Example 5: Create cache binding with field manager
curl -X POST /api/v1/namespaces/default/bindings?fieldManager=django-scheduler \
  -d '{
    "apiVersion": "v1",
    "kind": "Binding",
    "metadata": {"name": "cache-binding"},
    "target": {"kind": "Node", "name": "cache-node-5"}
  }'
Status: 201 Created Binding: cache-binding created Manager: django-scheduler

create create binding of a Pod

HTTP Request: POST /api/v1/namespaces/{namespace}/pods/{name}/binding

Creates a binding specifically for a Pod to assign it to a target Node in the cluster.

Full description

This operation creates a binding specifically for a named Pod, assigning it to a target Node. This is the primary mechanism used by the Kubernetes scheduler to place Django Pods on appropriate worker nodes. When you create a Pod binding, you're essentially telling Kubernetes which Node should run your Django application Pod. This operation is crucial for custom scheduling scenarios where you need to override the default scheduler behavior.

Syntax
POST /api/v1/namespaces/{namespace}/pods/{name}/binding
Content-Type: application/json

{
  "apiVersion": "v1",
  "kind": "Binding",
  "metadata": {
    "name": "pod-binding-name"
  },
  "target": {
    "apiVersion": "v1",
    "kind": "Node",
    "name": "target-node-name"
  }
}
Parameters description
  • name (path): Name of the Pod that will be bound to the target Node. Must match an existing Pod name in the namespace.
  • namespace (path): Namespace containing the Pod to be bound. Common values include "default", "production", or "staging".
  • body (required): Binding resource specification with target Node information for the Pod placement.
  • dryRun (query): Set to "All" to validate the binding request without actually binding the Pod to the Node.
  • fieldManager (query): Entity responsible for managing this binding operation, typically the scheduler name.
  • fieldValidation (query): Field validation strictness level for the binding request ("Ignore", "Warn", "Strict").
  • pretty (query): Format the API response for improved readability when set to "true".
Return value

HTTP Status Codes: 200 (OK), 201 (Created), 202 (Accepted), or 401 (Unauthorized) with the Pod binding confirmation.

5 short examples with Django models
Example 1: Bind Django web Pod
curl -X POST /api/v1/namespaces/default/pods/jobpost-web/binding \
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d '{
    "apiVersion": "v1",
    "kind": "Binding",
    "target": {"kind": "Node", "name": "web-server-node"}
  }'
Status: 201 Created Pod: jobpost-web bound to web-server-node Application: Django web server
Example 2: Bind Django API Pod with validation
curl -X POST /api/v1/namespaces/production/pods/jobpost-api/binding?dryRun=All \
  -d '{
    "apiVersion": "v1",
    "kind": "Binding",
    "target": {"kind": "Node", "name": "api-server-node"}
  }'
Status: 200 OK (dry run) Pod: jobpost-api binding validated Target: api-server-node (check passed)
Example 3: Bind Celery worker Pod
curl -X POST /api/v1/namespaces/default/pods/celery-worker/binding \
  -d '{
    "apiVersion": "v1",
    "kind": "Binding",
    "target": {"kind": "Node", "name": "worker-compute-node"}
  }'
Status: 201 Created Pod: celery-worker bound to worker-compute-node Purpose: Django background tasks
Example 4: Bind Django migration Pod
curl -X POST /api/v1/namespaces/staging/pods/django-migrate/binding \
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d '{
    "apiVersion": "v1",
    "kind": "Binding",
    "target": {"kind": "Node", "name": "database-node"}
  }'
Status: 201 Created Pod: django-migrate bound to database-node Task: Database schema migration
Example 5: Bind Redis cache Pod
curl -X POST /api/v1/namespaces/default/pods/redis-cache/binding?fieldManager=custom-scheduler \
  -d '{
    "apiVersion": "v1",
    "kind": "Binding",
    "target": {"kind": "Node", "name": "memory-optimized-node"}
  }'
Status: 201 Created Pod: redis-cache bound to memory-optimized-node Manager: custom-scheduler

PodTemplate

Docs: Kubernetes PodTemplate v1

PodTemplate describes a template for creating copies of a predefined pod.

📋 PodTemplate Components Overview

PodTemplate describes a template for creating copies of a predefined pod. This provides a reusable blueprint for Pod configurations that can be used across multiple Kubernetes resources.

For Django applications, PodTemplates are essential for creating consistent Pod configurations across different environments and deployments. They ensure that your Django web servers, API endpoints, worker processes, and background tasks all share the same base configuration, environment variables, resource limits, and security settings.

💡 Note: PodTemplates are typically embedded within higher-level controllers like Deployments, ReplicaSets, and StatefulSets rather than used as standalone resources. They provide consistency and reusability for Pod specifications across your Django application infrastructure.

🔧 Core Components

PodTemplate Object Reusable template for creating consistent Pod configurations
PodTemplateList Collection management for multiple Pod templates

🔧 PodTemplate Operations Overview

📋 Operation Categories

Create Operations Create new Pod templates with POST requests
Read Operations Query template information with GET requests
Update Operations Modify template configurations with PUT/PATCH
Delete Operations Remove Pod templates with DELETE requests
All Detailed Overviews Jump to complete component specifications

PodTemplate Overview

Docs: Kubernetes PodTemplate Resource

PodTemplate provides a reusable template for creating Pod configurations in Django applications.

Full description

📋 PodTemplate Fields Overview

apiVersion API version (v1) for PodTemplate resources
kind Resource type identifier (PodTemplate)
metadata Standard metadata with name, namespace, and labels
template Pod template specification with metadata and spec
template.metadata Metadata applied to Pods created from this template
template.spec Pod specification with containers, volumes, and configuration

PodTemplate is a Kubernetes resource that defines a template for creating multiple Pod instances with identical configurations. This is particularly useful for Django applications where you need to deploy multiple instances of the same service (web servers, API endpoints, or worker processes) with consistent settings. PodTemplates serve as blueprints that can be referenced by higher-level controllers like ReplicaSets, Deployments, and StatefulSets to ensure all Pod replicas share the same specification, environment variables, resource limits, and deployment parameters.

Syntax

apiVersion: v1
kind: PodTemplate
metadata:
  name: anihpj-web-template
  namespace: default
  labels:
    app: anihpj
    tier: web
template:
  metadata:
    labels:
      app: anihpj
      tier: web
  spec:
    containers:
    - name: django-web
      image: jobpost:v1.0
      ports:
      - containerPort: 8000
      env:
      - name: DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE
        value: "anihpj.settings.production"

Parameters description

  • apiVersion: Always "v1" for PodTemplate resources. Specifies the API version used for this template definition.
  • kind: Always "PodTemplate" to identify this as a Pod template resource type.
  • metadata: Standard object metadata including name, namespace, labels, and annotations for the PodTemplate itself.
  • template: The actual Pod template specification that defines how Pods should be created from this template.
  • template.metadata: Metadata that will be applied to Pods created from this template, including labels and annotations.
  • template.spec: The complete Pod specification including containers, volumes, security context, and scheduling constraints.

Return value

PodTemplate object: A reusable template specification for creating consistent Pod configurations in Django deployments.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Django web server template
apiVersion: v1
kind: PodTemplate
metadata:
  name: jobpost-web-template
template:
  spec:
    containers:
    - name: django-web
      image: jobpost-web:v2.0
      ports:
      - containerPort: 8000
PodTemplate: jobpost-web-template created Container: django-web on port 8000 Purpose: Django web application template
Example 2: Django API template with resources
apiVersion: v1
kind: PodTemplate
metadata:
  name: jobpost-api-template
template:
  spec:
    containers:
    - name: django-api
      image: jobpost-api:v1.5
      resources:
        requests:
          memory: "256Mi"
          cpu: "250m"
PodTemplate: jobpost-api-template created Resources: 256Mi memory, 250m CPU Type: Django REST API template
Example 3: Django Celery worker template
apiVersion: v1
kind: PodTemplate
metadata:
  name: celery-worker-template
template:
  spec:
    containers:
    - name: celery-worker
      image: jobpost-celery:v1.0
      command: ["celery", "-A", "anihpj", "worker"]
PodTemplate: celery-worker-template created Command: celery worker Purpose: Background task processing template
Example 4: Django with persistent storage template
apiVersion: v1
kind: PodTemplate
metadata:
  name: django-storage-template
template:
  spec:
    containers:
    - name: django-app
      image: jobpost:v1.0
      volumeMounts:
      - name: media-storage
        mountPath: /app/media
PodTemplate: django-storage-template created Volume: media-storage mounted at /app/media Purpose: Django with persistent media files
Example 5: Django with environment variables template
apiVersion: v1
kind: PodTemplate

  name: django-env-template

  spec:
    containers:
    - name: django-app
      image: jobpost:v1.0

PodTemplateSpec

Docs: Kubernetes PodTemplateSpec

PodTemplateSpec describes the data a pod should have when created from a template.

Full description

PodTemplateSpec is the core specification within a PodTemplate that defines exactly how Pods should be configured when created from the template. This specification includes all the metadata and Pod spec details that will be applied to each Pod instance. For Django applications, this is where you define container images, environment variables, resource requirements, volume mounts, and other Pod-level configurations that ensure consistent deployment of your Django services across multiple replicas.

Syntax

template:
  metadata:
    labels:
      app: anihpj
      component: web
      version: v1.0
  spec:
    containers:
    - name: anihpj-web
      image: anihpj-web:v1.0
      ports:
      - containerPort: 8000
        name: http
      env:
      - name: DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE
        value: "anihpj.settings.production"
      resources:
        requests:
          memory: "512Mi"
          cpu: "500m"
        limits:
          memory: "1Gi"
          cpu: "1000m"

Parameters description

  • metadata: Standard object metadata that will be applied to Pods created from this template, including labels for service discovery and annotations for configuration.
  • spec: Complete Pod specification defining containers, volumes, security context, scheduling constraints, and other Pod-level configurations.
  • metadata.labels: Key-value pairs used for Pod identification, service selection, and resource grouping in Django deployments.
  • spec.containers: Array of container specifications that define the Django application containers and their configurations.
  • spec.volumes: Volume definitions for persistent storage, configuration files, and shared data between containers.
  • spec.restartPolicy: Policy for restarting containers within the Pod ("Always", "OnFailure", or "Never").

Return value

PodTemplateSpec object: Complete Pod configuration template used by controllers to create identical Pod instances.

5 short examples with Django models

Example 1: Basic Django web spec
template:
  metadata:
    labels:
      app: anihpj-web
  spec:
    containers:
    - name: anihpj
      image: anihpj:v1.0
      ports:
      - containerPort: 8000
PodTemplateSpec: Basic Django web configuration Port: 8000 exposed Labels: app=jobpost-web
Example 2: Django with resource limits spec
template:
  spec:
    containers:
    - name: django-api
      image: jobpost-api:v1.0
      resources:
        requests:
          memory: "256Mi"
        limits:
          memory: "512Mi"
PodTemplateSpec: Django API with resources Memory: 256Mi request, 512Mi limit Container: django-api
Example 3: Django with volumes spec
template:
  spec:
    containers:
    - name: django
      image: jobpost:v1.0
      volumeMounts:
      - name: static-files
        mountPath: /app/static
    volumes:
    - name: static-files
      emptyDir: {}
PodTemplateSpec: Django with volume mount Volume: static-files at /app/static Type: emptyDir volume
Example 4: Django with multiple containers spec
template:
  spec:
    containers:
    - name: django-web
      image: jobpost-web:v1.0
      ports:
      - containerPort: 8000
    - name: nginx-proxy
      image: nginx:alpine
      ports:
      - containerPort: 80
PodTemplateSpec: Multi-container Django Pod Containers: django-web + nginx-proxy Ports: 8000 (Django), 80 (Nginx)
Example 5: Django with security context spec
template:
  spec:
    securityContext:
      runAsUser: 1000
      runAsGroup: 3000
    containers:
    - name: django
      image: jobpost:v1.0
      securityContext:
        allowPrivilegeEscalation: false
PodTemplateSpec: Django with security context User: 1000, Group: 3000 Security: No privilege escalation

PodTemplateList

Docs: Kubernetes PodTemplateList

PodTemplateList is a list of PodTemplates.

Full description

PodTemplateList is a collection resource that contains multiple PodTemplate objects. This is typically returned when you query the Kubernetes API for multiple PodTemplates using list operations. In Django deployment scenarios, you might have multiple PodTemplates for different services (web servers, API endpoints, background workers, database migrations) and PodTemplateList helps you manage and iterate through these templates programmatically or via kubectl commands.

Syntax

apiVersion: v1
kind: PodTemplateList
metadata:
  resourceVersion: "12345"
items:
- apiVersion: v1
  kind: PodTemplate
  metadata:
    name: jobpost-web-template
  template:
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: django-web
        image: jobpost-web:v1.0
- apiVersion: v1
  kind: PodTemplate
  metadata:
    name: jobpost-api-template
  template:
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: django-api
        image: jobpost-api:v1.0

Parameters description

  • apiVersion: Always "v1" for PodTemplateList resources.
  • kind: Always "PodTemplateList" to identify this as a list of PodTemplate resources.
  • metadata: Standard list metadata including resource version for consistency and pagination support.
  • items: Array containing individual PodTemplate objects, each with complete template specifications.
  • metadata.resourceVersion: Version identifier used for consistent reads and watch operations across the cluster.
  • items[].template: Each item contains a complete PodTemplateSpec defining Pod creation parameters.

Return value

PodTemplateList object: Collection of PodTemplate resources with metadata for list operations and pagination.

5 short examples with Django models
